SOCIAL WELFARE 


There is, of course, no real 
answer to Father Ryan’s 
penctrative analysis of what 
is wrong with the — social 
structure of Hong Kong. At 
least, there could not be were 
this Colony situated in any 
other part of the globe than 
the China coast, and so close 
that to talk, as did the 
YMCA Debating Group the 
same evening, of restricting 
the immipration of Chinese 
secking refuye, is to close all 
eyes to the realities. Give 
us the benefits of being a 
self-contained community, 
free to admit or exclude 
those useful as citizens, or 
unwanted, as the case might 
be, and Father Ryan's appeal 
would be irresistible, We 
all know, or most of us, how 
disgracefully far behind we 
are. We all know why pri- 
vilege remains ensconced. 
Or most of us. The appal- 
ling housing conditions of 
the poor; the absence of 
educational facilities for 
thousands of ‘children; the 
hopeless inadequacy of medi- 
cal care for those in great- 
est need who are without re- 
sources: in peneral, the rela- 
tive indifference to the well- 
being of the great mass. of 
Hone Kong's community, are 
all too apparent, This, in- 
decd, is part of the trouble. 
So much of misery, of suffer- 
ing and_ distress. of wretch- 
edness and desolation, is 
about, us that it is the un- 
fortunate tendency to be- 
come inured, and to take re- 
fuge in what comfort can bo 
derived from the saying that 
“the poor are alwavs with 
us." From this attitude of 
.mind, or closing of the mind, 
Father Ryan's lecture was 
designed to shake us, and it 
will have achieved its pur- 
pose if it accomplishes nu 
more than to compel us, 

| (meaning the community and 
the government) to a re- 
examination of our approach 
and our consciences. Frank- 
ly, and Father Ryan spoke 
frankly enough, _we would 
say that there was too much 
missing in his exposition of 
the reauirements. Nothing 
was said, of any moment, 
about the cost of the pro- 
eramme. No hint was given 
of the method by which this 
Colony would deal with the 
bugbear’ which appears im- 
mediately any suggestion 3s 
made of extending social 
welfare commitments — the 
certainty of a heavy influx 
of Chinese from the hinter- 
land only too willing to par- 
ticipate in their benefits. 
Father Ryan would doubtless 
dismiss this instinctive re- 
sistance as representing the 
mentality of 50 years ago, 
but he too. in his analysis 
dealt with what he called the 
theoretical and the practical. 
Theoreticafly, all possessed 
with a social conscience must 
a wholehearted with 
everything that Father, Ryan 
urged. Practically, . they 
must comprumise and do the 
best they can under ‘existing 
conditions. : :

guard “against persons. -pre-

will ordinarily be measured in 
the boldness, almost foolhardl- 
acess, of his movements.. The 
whole purpose of the ‘costly 
brenk-through anct the whirlwind 
attacks of the succeeding three 


hweeks wis to praduce just such 


fn rittationsias now confronted us: 
we had been preparing cur plans 
so as to reap the richest harvest 
from the initial success, But the 
dicultles of supply, onee our 
colum began thelr forward 
race, wisn problem that required 
effective solution if we were to 
fain our full battle protit. 

Our logistic formations | had 
been confined in oa very restricted 
aren during the entlre Battle of 
the Beachhead, The only operat- 
ing, ports were Cherbourg und 
the artificial part on the British 
beaches new Arromanches, The 
repair of Cherbourg had present. 
ed mans difficulties, The har- 
hour and approaches had to be 
cleare| of hundreds of mines, 
many of them of new and parti- 
cularky efficient types, We began 
using the port in July, but it did 
not rench volume = production 
until the middle of August. The 
artificial pert oon the American 
benches had been demolished in 
the June storm, From Arromun- 
ches and Cherbourg we had not 
been able to project forward the 
rondn, rallways, and dumpa as we 
would have done had our breuts- 
out line actually been as far to 
the Southward: as the base of the 
Cotentin Peninsula, where we 
oripinally expected it to be. All 
our marching columns, therefore, 
had to be supplied from. stocks 
located: near the — beaches anit 
Jover coads and rathways that had 
to be repaired as we advanced. 

These mente — facilities could 
not support ux Indefnitely and 
there was bound to be oa ine 
somewhere in the direction of 
Germany where we would be 
halted, if not by the action of the 
enemy, then because our supply 
nes had been strained to their 
elastic limit. | 

A reinforced division, in active 
operutions, consumes from 600 to 
700 tons of supplies pee day. 
When battling in a Axed position, 
most of this tonnage Is represent- 
ed in ammunition; on the march 
the bulk Is devoted to gasoline 
and lubricants, called, n-th? 
language of the supply officer, 
POL. 


With thirty-six divisions in ace 
tlon we were faced with the pro- 
blem of delivering from beaches 
nnd ports to the front lines some 
20,000 tons of supplics every day, 
Our spearhends, moreover, were 
moving swiftly, frequently 
seventy-five miles per day. Thi 
supply service had to cntch these 
with loaded trucks. Every mile 
of advance doubled the diMculty 
because the supply truck had al- 
ways to make a two-way run to 
the beaches and back, in order to 
deliver another lond to the march- 
ing troops. Other thousands of 
tons had to go Into advanced aire 
flelds for construction and subse~ 
quent. malntenanee,-.-- Still -addi- 
tional amounts were required for 
repair of bridges and roads, for 
which henvy equipment was 
necessary, 

During the duys that we were 
raped off in the beachhead wo 
could not foresee the exact reac- 
tlon of the enemy following upon 
a successful breakout on our 
right, His most logical move up- 
peared to be a awinging of his 
troops back toward the Scine, to 
defend the crossings of that river. 
If he had chosen to do this he 
could undoubtedly have’ made a 
stubborn defence of that obstacle 
until our advancing troops were 
able to outflank him and force 
evacuation. 

If we had been compelled to 
fight a general battle on the Seine 
our lines of communication would 
have been relatively short and 
the logistic problem would have 
been solved gradually, conform- 
ing to the pace that our . own 
troops could advance., However, 
when the enemy decided, under 
Hitler's insistence, to stand where 
he wos and to counterattack 
against the flank of aur marching 


columns at Mortain§ the entire |" 


prospect was changed,

portunity to” swing in from the

enmpalgn to determine what 
major changes this new develop- 
Ment would Indicate as deslrable,

ities then presented to us were 
the carly capture of Marseille, far 
in the South, and Antwerp, In 
Belgium. Possession of this Int- 
ter port, I{ usable, would solve 
our toglstic problems for the 
entire Northern half of our front,

Doctor Helpless 
In Face Of Gale 


London, January 5. 


Dr. Hugh Loye, of Patrington, near Hull, stood ona 
‘ galc-swopt beach at the mouth of the Humber 
within a fow yards of his patient but was unable 


to hefp him. 

His only protection’ from the 
vale was a borrowed beret and 
as soldver’s. gas cape, His 
patient was William Helt, aged 
47, of Grimsby, onv of the crew 
of 11 of the trawler Deveron, 

Belt was’ serlously Injured 
during the gale when caught 
between two hawners four days 
out from Grimsby. The vzasel 
was headed for home: at full 
speed but ran aground, 

An SOS for the — cloctor was 
sent by hand signal (rom the 
trawler, but the local doctor 
could not be reached as the gale 
had blown down telephone wires, 


Dr Love, brought from Hull 
Inca police car, waded time and 
aguin into Une sea but was driven 
Dack by the surf. Eventually, 
after two huurs, he reached the 
trawler ound was hauled on 
board. 

The doctor and a fisherman 
Jashvd Belt in a blanket and a 
linen sling. They were‘ unable to 
pass him through the narrow 
companionway, so the chief en- 
gineer broke the doorway’ down. 


Balt was then lowered over the 
side to the beach—the rising tide 
baing beaten by a few minutes.

Commended By U.S.

Mr. Robert Lovett, Acting Sccretary of State, sent 
messages today to the Primo Ministers of India 

and Pakistan commending them for their

Jammu and Kashmir, was a 
sound basis tor settlement, and 
praised the promptness of the

the cease-fire called for by the 
United Nations Commission for 
India and Pakistan.

worded to both governments, 
sald? :

learn of the acceptance by your

fencral acclaim for the action of

awaiting formal action by the

| Spread: OF Heroin 


Success, January 5. 


Tho United Nations Permanent Central Opium 
Board expresses concern over the alarming in- 
¢roaso in world uso of horoin and says doctors 
aro to blame in many countrics, : 

In a report to United Nations Headquarters from 
Geneva, the Board says that 12 of 22 countries 
which are the heaviest users of heroin have; 
raised consumption since 1936, 


It gays the five higher per 
capita users in the world are 
Finland, Italy, New Zealand, 
Sweden and Australia. 


The report says Finland’s con~ 
sumption in 1947 was 300 per 
cent greater: that in 1936, while 
Italy is using 50 per cent more, or 
seven times the per capita con- 
sumption of France, where the 
quantity consumed hos dropped 
to one-half, 


“The drug is popular with 
medical men in some countries 
because It is simple to administer 
and quickly alleviates paln and 
cough,” the report continues, 


“On account of its habit- 
forming power, doctors in many 
countries have scught and found 
eMcjent and comparatively harm- 
less substitutes. 


Doctors Lax 


“The Board believes doctors 
In certain other countries may 
either be Ineufficiently  ac- 
quainted with theoe tesults of 
_modern research or else far too 
tax In regard to the dangerous 
consequences’ that may follow 
the prescription of heroin.” 


It says the’ problem Is so serlous 
that the Board has referred the 
matter to the world Health Or- 
gonisation, whose experts on 
norcotle drugs are to micet fate 
this month jn Geneva, 


The Board polnts out that In 
25 countries, including the United 
States and American-controlied 
Japan, heroin is not used at all, 
—United Press, $

This Nose Does 
Your Breathing 


St. Louls, January 6. 


Anew plastic nose, 


that docs your breathing for you, 


was shown to the American Medicat Associa- - 


tion. 


This nose ‘réplaces the iron 
lung and often does the same 
life saving job. It is a small 
gadget, making an airtight . fit 
aver the nose and mouth. Its 
secret is a v artime valve, by 
which {t can be attached to a 
tube carrying pure oxygen, or 
any other mixture a doctor 
wants.. : . 


The value ‘automatically puts 
on pressure enough to force ulr 
Into the lungs, and then relaxes 
for nir to flow out, ,The doctor 
eon set it for whatever breath- 
ing rate is wanted. : 


Breathing 1s effortless. Un~ 
consclous people, whose lungs 
have stopped working,, breathe 
naturally. 7 ’ 

The nose .is named pneophare, 
which means ‘artificlal’ resplira- 
tion, It fs. made by the Min: 
[Safety ‘Appliances Company at 
Pittsburgh. It was developed 


+ . 


first for miners, but.is also usec |° 


in infantile parolyals, drownine 
roverdoses of slecping pills, for 
mothers: of . premature babies 
asthma, and: In one mental hos- 
pital for patients, who p2ss ,out 
luring shock treatment. » 
Penlelliin. - atrantomycin and 
‘other medlolnes, ‘t “ghe . forr, 
of mists. are Used In the auto: 
i matle Greathen 2, 


The medical ‘develupment was 
done by Dr, Hurley L. Motley, 
Barton Memorlal Division of the 
Jefferson Medical College, 
Philadelphia, -and Drs. Lars 
Werko, Andre  Counand and 
Dickinson - W. Richards, Jr. 
Columbia University 
School apd Bellevue Hospital.

their lungs that resemble tuber- |. 


culosis, ofter breathing with this 
nose for a short time daily for

Old Chinese 
Music At 
Y’s- Men’s Meet. 


Music. that.. was. .onee.. royal 
entertainment in .mandarin 
courts was played by Mr. 
Loyis Chen Po-tung on an an- 
elent Chinese harp at the 
weekly luncheon of the Inter- 
national Y's Men's Club at 
Hong Kong Hotel yesterday. 

The weileknown Teochew ex- 

vt of the ancient 18-string 
Chinese instrument {y on a_tour 
of the South before leaving China 
tor Europe and the United States. 
Mr. Chen expects to depart for 
France for further study in ar- 
cheoloyy in the University of 
Paris by the tend of this month. 
During his sojourn there ho ex- 
pects to give a serles of concerts, 

Among his special selection of 
clnssics that were once favourites 
of Chinese officials,’ were “Penls 
of the Hungry Horse,” “Entering 
the Palace” and “Rain on the 
Banona Leaves," Each pleco was 
played with the touch of a well- 
experienced ar complete with 
the familiar crescendo interpret- 
Ing heightened emotion. Some of 
the selections, however, cnded 
tranquilly, especlally Mr. Chon’s 
version of “Ruin on.the Banana 
Leaves” Which he finished with 
“echoes,” i 

The Chinese harp, measuring 
about five feet long with a faut- 
wide bridge tapering to six inches 
at the other end, dated - aoa far 
buck ns Confuclus’ days, somo 
2,000 yenrs ago. The Mongol in- 
vasion relegated the urt to obll- 
vion, and it is the hope of Mr. 
Chen to revive it in Ching, pri- 
marily, then abroad. 

Eighteen conical wooden» pegs 
nre ‘placed diagonally .a¢ross the 
nlywood-board to provide four 
Chineso music scales, The boar 
is fretless, and by light pressure 


with the right ‘fluers the Harplat | yo 


derives his “sharps”, ond “flats.” 
By thls means also he can produce 
extra scales’ from any two strings. 
A atudent of urchcology’ at the 
Yonching University, -‘Mr.- Chen 
beyon playing the harp about 18 
years ago. -He graduated from 
the. Chungshan University, Can- 
ton, before golng up North. Mr, 
Chen hails from Swatow. 4 
' During. his visfls ‘to Singapore, 
Penong,. Slam and several Malay 
states, he made recordings’ of 
rome classics, He gave! a series 
of ' concerts, ‘sponsored. by. ‘the 
Chinese Consuls of. the sen) ve 
elfles.' ‘Mr, Chen broadcast : from

Italians Flee . 
From Ear 


An earthquake shook the Ricti area, North of Rome, 
at 5.00 a.m. local time today, killing two people 
and damaging several huridred homes. 


The most serious duminge was 
dorie in the village of Rivedu- 
tri where 95 per cent. of the 
houses were reported damaged. 
Several collapsed. 

Tonight, the Inhabitants were 
fieding to the bits as the hour 
of the predieted earthquake dis- 
uster approached, 

The predictlon was credited 
to a Londoner, Me. Willlam 
Wheeler. 

‘Telephone calls tonight were 
pouring? Into newspaper offices 
und observatories despite assur- 
ances from Iillan geological ox- 
perts that earthquakes cannot be 
predicted. 

The Rieti and Sabine areas 
have for the past week experi- 
enced. earth tretnors which the 
veople believe to be the forerun- 
ners of bigger shocks te come.

Priests Arrive 
To Help — 


Salesians 


Four Roman Catholic priests 
arrived here yesterday by the 
Dutch steamer Meerkerk from 
Europe: - viatManila to ‘help 
with the work of the Salesian 
Society in the Far Enst. 

Father Bonfanti, hailing from 
Italy, Js joining the Sulestan House 
of Studies, Shaukiwan, to teach 
sclence. A. 20-yenr-old brother 
is going to Shanghaj to teach car- 
pentry, A Spanish brother Js 
leaving for Macgo this week-end 
to foln the mission there, Another 
Catholic priest is recovering from 
sensickness on the arrival of the 
Meerkerk. He is’ In transit to 
Japan, 

Two Dutch Salesian fathers are 
expected -to---reach'-the --Colony. 
very soon to join the group here, 
it was learned yesterday. Thoy 
pro, meanwhile, owalting trann~ 
port in Europe. : ; 

The missfon sends about. 300 
priesis to the all parts af the 
world . yeorly. Father Bonfantt 
ls the ‘tirst be added to the 
local group for 1049. 

The Meerkerk ‘left Maniin on 
January 4 and had oa stormy 20- 
hour voyage en route. The vessel 
encountered’ strong: North Easter-
